# Volume VI — Agent and Subagent Architecture

## 1. Why Agent/Subagent Architecture Is Necessary

ION cannot scale by asking one agent to hold the whole project in active context.

The project has become too large, too layered, and too authority-sensitive for a single context window or a small fixed set of major agents to reliably maintain:

```text
constitutional law
runtime modules
template contracts
front-door behavior
release evidence
production readiness
graph canon
source-summary rewrite authority
agent activation law
daemon safety
historical donor lineage
current truenames
known contradictions
open production gaps
```

ION’s answer is not merely “more context.” Its answer is **lawful context distribution**.

The living context graph should be divided into graph regions, contextual nodes, domain surfaces, workflow surfaces, release surfaces, and evidence surfaces. Specialist agents and subagents may operate over bounded regions, but only through formal context packages, leases, receipts, review paths, and kill switches.

The goal is not an uncontrolled swarm.

The goal is:

```text
bounded distributed cognition over a governed living context graph
```

---

## 2. The Core Scaling Principle

The agent/subagent architecture rests on one production principle:

```text
ION scales context by distributing graph-region custody across lawful specialist agents and subagents whose inputs, outputs, leases, permissions, and receipts are template-bound.
```

This is different from ordinary multi-agent orchestration.

Ordinary multi-agent systems often do this:

```text
spawn agents
give each a task
collect answers
merge answers by prompt judgment
```

ION must do this:

```text
identify graph region
compile context package
define rights
issue lease
spawn agent/subagent
receive result receipt
validate against graph canon and authority
fan-in through review
commit only through lawful surfaces
```

The difference is the graph.

Agents do not merely answer. They operate over graph regions.

---

## 3. Agent Layers

Production ION should define at least five agent layers.

```text
Persona Interface
Relay
Steward / primary orchestrator
domain manager agents
specialist subagents
contextual node workers
```

These are not all peers. They have different authority, context, and obligations.

---

## 4. Persona Interface

## 4.1 Role

Persona Interface is the user-facing graph projection renderer.

It translates system state into a human-usable response without becoming the graph-native orchestrator.

## 4.2 Rights

```text
read selected return packages
render user-facing response
ask clarification where allowed
surface review needs
show evidence and boundaries
```

## 4.3 Forbidden

```text
canonical graph write
source-summary rewrite
agent activation
subagent spawn
authority ratification
```

## 4.4 Production rule

```text
Persona Interface does not become Steward.
```

This prevents user-facing language from becoming runtime authority.

---

## 5. Relay

## 5.1 Role

Relay is the semantic-boundary translator.

It converts between:

```text
human-facing intent
graph-native packets
runtime outputs
user-facing responses
```

## 5.2 Rights

```text
semantic translation
packet framing
boundary preservation
return packaging
```

## 5.3 Forbidden

```text
final orchestration authority
canonical graph write
agent activation without Steward/authority gate
source rewrite
```

## 5.4 Production rule

```text
Relay translates across boundaries; it does not dissolve them.
```

---

## 6. Steward / Primary Orchestrator

## 6.1 Role

Steward is the current orchestration truename.

Steward routes graph-native work, decides which workflow or domain should handle a request, and may propose fan-out when production activation law exists.

## 6.2 Rights before V39–V40

Before agent activation authority exists, Steward may:

```text
route bounded workflows
create proposal packets
call existing certified/replay/doctor/release commands where allowed
request human review
draft subagent activation proposals
```

Steward may not:

```text
autonomously spawn subagents
grant graph-region custody
grant canonical write
grant source rewrite
bypass graph canon
```

## 6.3 Production rule

```text
Steward can propose fan-out before V39–V40.
Steward cannot autonomously execute fan-out before activation law.
```

---

## 7. Domain Manager Agents

## 7.1 Role

Domain manager agents oversee coherent graph regions.

Examples:

```text
Graph Canon Manager
Source Summary Rewrite Manager
Release Engineering Manager
Agent Activation Manager
Daemon Runtime Manager
Front-Door Product Manager
Historical Lineage Manager
Template Contract Manager
```

## 7.2 Responsibilities

A domain manager may eventually:

```text
maintain domain context packages
detect stale nodes
request specialist subagents
review subagent outputs
propose graph updates
surface contradictions
prepare evidence bundles
```

## 7.3 Rights before ratification

Before production activation law:

```text
domain manager = role concept / planning surface
```

It may be described and planned, but not autonomously activated with graph-region custody.

## 7.4 Production rule

```text
Domain managers require graph-region definition, lease authority, and result receipt schema.
```

---

## 8. Specialist Subagents

## 8.1 Role

Specialist subagents perform bounded work inside a domain.

Examples:

```text
Protocol Auditor
Registry Auditor
Runtime Test Reader
Source Provenance Extractor
Contradiction Scanner
Alias/Truename Auditor
Release Evidence Verifier
Graph Edge Class Proposer
Summary Patch Reviewer
Daemon Failure Mode Analyst
```

## 8.2 Required input

A specialist subagent should receive:

```text
task packet
context package
graph region
allowed surfaces
forbidden surfaces
expected output schema
lease duration
receipt requirements
escalation rules
```

## 8.3 Required output

A specialist subagent must return:

```text
result receipt
claim list
source references
uncertainties
contradictions
proposed changes
forbidden actions not taken
next recommended action
```

## 8.4 Production rule

```text
A subagent without a result receipt did not lawfully act.
```

---

## 9. Contextual Node Workers

## 9.1 Role

Contextual node workers are the smallest agentic units.

They operate over narrow graph-node or graph-edge clusters.

Example tasks:

```text
inspect one protocol and extract authority claims
compare one historical alias against current truename
summarize one receipt family
verify one registry row
check one test output against a production claim
extract provenance for one summary patch
```

## 9.2 Why they matter

ION’s context cannot be globally loaded. It must be locally perfect.

A contextual node worker may hold:

```text
one node
its immediate edges
relevant provenance
one task template
one output schema
```

This allows high-fidelity local work without polluting the global orchestrator context.

## 9.3 Production rule

```text
Contextual node workers do not decide global truth.
```

They return local evidence to higher fan-in review.

---

## 10. Fan-Out/Fan-In Orchestration

## 10.1 Fan-out

Fan-out means decomposing a graph-region task into bounded sub-tasks.

Example:

```text
Steward receives A3 ratification task
→ Domain Manager divides surfaces by family
→ subagents inspect protocol, registry, tests, receipts
→ contextual workers extract local evidence
```

## 10.2 Fan-in

Fan-in means recombining subagent outputs lawfully.

Fan-in must include:

```text
schema validation
evidence check
contradiction scan
confidence comparison
duplicate claim merge
unresolved issue list
review verdict
receipt
```

## 10.3 Production rule

```text
Fan-in is not averaging.
```

It is governed evidence reconciliation.

---

## 11. Agent Context Packages

## 11.1 Definition

An agent context package is a bounded graph-derived context packet issued to an agent or subagent.

It should include:

```yaml
agent_context_package:
  package_id:
  target_agent:
  issuing_authority:
  graph_region:
  task_id:
  allowed_sources: []
  forbidden_sources: []
  current_claims: []
  authority_boundaries: []
  relevant_receipts: []
  relevant_tests: []
  open_questions: []
  known_contradictions: []
  output_schema:
  lease:
  kill_switch:
```

## 11.2 Required properties

```text
bounded
provenance-aware
authority-aware
staleness-aware
receipt-backed
lease-bound
```

## 11.3 Production rule

```text
No agent should receive raw broad context when a graph-region context package is required.
```

---

## 12. Agent Leases

## 12.1 Definition

A lease defines the temporary authority under which an agent operates.

Lease fields:

```yaml
agent_lease:
  lease_id:
  agent_id:
  issued_by:
  issued_at:
  expires_at:
  graph_region:
  allowed_actions: []
  forbidden_actions: []
  read_rights: []
  proposal_rights: []
  review_rights: []
  commit_rights: []
  source_rewrite_rights: false
  agent_spawn_rights: false
  heartbeat_interval:
  kill_switch:
```

## 12.2 Production rule

```text
No lease, no activation.
```

## 12.3 Expiration

When a lease expires:

```text
agent stops acting
unfinished work returns as partial receipt
context package marked stale or expired
fan-in receives incomplete status
```

---

## 13. Heartbeats

## 13.1 Definition

A heartbeat proves an agent is still operating within lease.

Heartbeat record:

```yaml
agent_heartbeat:
  heartbeat_id:
  lease_id:
  agent_id:
  timestamp:
  current_phase:
  last_action:
  open_questions: []
  detected_risks: []
  needs_extension: false
```

## 13.2 Production rule

```text
A silent agent cannot retain active authority indefinitely.
```

---

## 14. Result Receipts

## 14.1 Definition

A result receipt is the formal return from an agent or subagent.

Receipt schema:

```yaml
agent_result_receipt:
  receipt_id:
  agent_id:
  lease_id:
  context_package_id:
  task_id:
  status:
    - COMPLETE
    - PARTIAL
    - BLOCKED
    - ESCALATED
    - FAILED
  claims: []
  evidence_refs: []
  proposed_changes: []
  contradictions: []
  uncertainties: []
  forbidden_actions_not_taken: []
  next_actions: []
  output_hash:
```

## 14.2 Production rule

```text
No receipt, no fan-in.
```

The orchestrator may not silently merge unreceipted agent output.

---

## 15. Kill Switches

## 15.1 Definition

A kill switch revokes an agent lease or stops a fan-out branch.

Kill-switch triggers:

```text
lease expiration
forbidden action attempted
contradiction severity critical
source context stale
graph region locked
human stop
budget exceeded
heartbeat missed
agent output violates schema
```

## 15.2 Kill-switch record

```yaml
agent_kill_switch_event:
  event_id:
  lease_id:
  agent_id:
  triggered_by:
  reason:
  severity:
  stopped_at:
  partial_receipt_required: true
  follow_up:
```

## 15.3 Production rule

```text
Any production agent activation system without a kill switch is unsafe.
```

---

## 16. Conflict Escalation

## 16.1 Agent conflict

Agent conflict occurs when agents return incompatible claims.

Example:

```text
Subagent A says surface is A2-ready.
Subagent B says same surface is A4 demo-only.
```

## 16.2 Required handling

```text
do not average claims
preserve both receipts
classify contradiction
request source comparison
escalate to reviewer if authority-impacting
```

Conflict record:

```yaml
agent_conflict_record:
  conflict_id:
  involved_receipts: []
  conflict_type:
  affected_surface:
  severity:
  proposed_resolution:
  required_reviewer:
```

## 16.3 Production rule

```text
Contradictory agent outputs must not be smoothed into one confident answer.
```

---

## 17. Contextual Node Graph Architecture

The contextual node architecture is the practical substrate for distributed context.

## 17.1 Node custody

Each contextual node can have:

```text
current summary
source refs
edge list
authority class
staleness status
assigned manager
recent receipts
open contradictions
```

## 17.2 Worker assignment

A contextual worker may be assigned to:

```text
one node
one edge family
one graph-region slice
one source/provenance cluster
one contradiction cluster
```

## 17.3 Fan-in target

Worker outputs flow to:

```text
specialist subagent
domain manager
Steward
review surface
bounded commit proposal
```

## 17.4 Production rule

```text
Local node custody is not global authority.
```

---

## 18. Graph-Region Custody

Agent activation depends on graph-region custody.

Graph-region custody defines:

```text
who may read region
who may propose changes
who may review changes
who may commit bounded changes
who may request subagents
who may escalate
who may mark region stale
```

Custody record:

```yaml
graph_region_custody:
  region_id:
  custodian_agent:
  custody_scope:
  read_rights: []
  proposal_rights: []
  review_rights: []
  bounded_commit_rights: []
  canonical_write_rights: []
  subagent_spawn_rights: []
  lease_required: true
```

Production rule:

```text
No graph-region custody without graph-region registry and lease.
```

---

## 19. Agent Activation Request

Activation request schema:

```yaml
agent_activation_request:
  request_id:
  requested_agent:
  requested_by:
  reason:
  graph_region:
  task_template:
  context_package_required: true
  requested_rights:
    read: []
    propose: []
    review: []
    bounded_commit: []
    canonical_write: false
    source_rewrite: false
    spawn_subagents: false
  expected_outputs: []
  lease_duration:
  kill_switch:
  review_required: true
```

Activation review verdicts:

```text
APPROVE
HOLD
ESCALATE
REJECT
NEEDS_CONTEXT_PACKAGE
NEEDS_GRAPH_REGION
NEEDS_HUMAN_APPROVAL
```

Production rule:

```text
Activation requests are proposals until reviewed.
```

---

## 20. Agent Activation Rights

Write/activation classes:

```text
A0 no activation
A1 draft activation proposal
A2 reviewed activation request
A3 activate single specialist agent with read/proposal rights
A4 activate bounded subagent fan-out with no commit rights
A5 activate agent with review rights
A6 activate agent with bounded commit rights
A7 activate agent with canonical write or source rewrite rights
```

Current state after V33:

```text
A0 allowed
A1 allowed
A2 proposal/review only
A3+ blocked
A7 forbidden until far future and likely human-gated
```

Production rule:

```text
A7 should never be granted without explicit human and constitutional review.
```

---

## 21. Subagent Spawn Protocol

A subagent spawn must include:

```text
parent agent
spawn reason
task packet
context package
lease
output schema
budget
heartbeat
kill switch
fan-in target
```

Spawn record:

```yaml
subagent_spawn_record:
  spawn_id:
  parent_agent:
  child_agent:
  context_package_id:
  lease_id:
  task_id:
  fan_in_target:
  spawned_at:
  expires_at:
```

Production rule:

```text
Subagents may not recursively spawn without explicit spawn-depth authority.
```

---

## 22. Fan-Out Budget and Depth

ION should define fan-out constraints.

Budget fields:

```yaml
fanout_budget:
  max_subagents:
  max_depth:
  max_runtime:
  max_tokens_or_compute:
  max_files:
  max_graph_nodes:
  max_retry_count:
```

Production rule:

```text
No unbounded recursive agent fan-out.
```

---

## 23. Human Review and Sovereign Authority

Certain activations require human review.

Examples:

```text
canonical write rights
source rewrite rights
agent spawn rights beyond bounded depth
constitutional surfaces
authority ratification
production release gates
sensitive-topic access
```

Production rule:

```text
Human/Sovereign review remains final for high-authority transitions.
```

---

## 24. Relationship to Graph Canon

Agent activation depends on graph canon.

An agent cannot safely know its boundaries unless the graph has:

```text
regions
node classes
edge classes
write classes
custody rules
conflict handling
rollback semantics
```

Therefore:

```text
V36 Graph Canon precedes V39–V40 Agent Activation.
```

Production rule:

```text
No production graph-region custody without graph canon.
```

---

## 25. Relationship to Source Summary Rewrite

Agent context packages depend on current summaries.

If summaries change:

```text
agent context packages may become stale
active leases may require review
subagent outputs may need reinterpretation
fan-in may need contradiction scan
```

Therefore:

```text
source-summary rewrite must notify dependent agent context packages.
```

Production rule:

```text
Summary mutation can invalidate agent context.
```

---

## 26. Relationship to Daemon Runtime

The daemon may eventually execute activation requests, heartbeats, expirations, and fan-in.

But daemon activation of agents is high-risk.

Production rule:

```text
Daemon may not activate agents until agent activation law exists and daemon safe mode/queue locks are proven.
```

This places agent activation and daemon runtime in strict order.

---

V39 should create agent activation authority without broad fan-out runtime.

```text
ION/02_architecture/AGENT_ACTIVATION_AUTHORITY_PROTOCOL.md
```

V40 should add bounded subagent fan-out/fan-in runtime.

```text
ION/02_architecture/SUBAGENT_SPAWN_PROTOCOL.md

ION/04_packages/kernel/agent_conflict_escalation.py
ION/tests/test_kernel_subagent_spawn.py
ION/tests/test_kernel_fanout_orchestrator.py
ION/tests/test_kernel_fanin_review.py
ION/tests/test_kernel_agent_result_receipt.py
ION/tests/test_kernel_agent_conflict_escalation.py
```

V40 should prove bounded fan-out/fan-in, not unlimited autonomous swarms.

---

## 29. Acceptance Criteria

Agent/subagent architecture is minimally production-candidate when:

```text
1. activation requests are proposal objects
2. context packages are required
3. leases are required
4. heartbeats are required
5. kill switches are required
6. result receipts are required
7. fan-in validates schema and evidence
8. conflicts become explicit records
9. subagent spawn depth is bounded
10. graph-region custody depends on graph canon
11. summary mutation invalidates dependent context packages
12. unauthorized activation is refused by tests
13. no agent receives canonical write or source rewrite rights by default
```

---

## 30. Anti-Patterns

## 30.1 Unbounded swarm

Bad:

```text
spawn as many agents as useful until the task seems done
```

Correct:

```text
spawn bounded subagents under lease, budget, context package, heartbeat, and receipt law
```

## 30.2 Context dump

Bad:

```text
give subagent the whole project and ask it to figure it out
```

Correct:

```text
compile graph-region context package with authority boundaries and output schema
```

## 30.3 Silent fan-in

Bad:

```text
merge subagent outputs into final answer without preserving receipts
```

Correct:

```text
fan-in validates, compares, records contradictions, and emits reviewable synthesis
```

## 30.4 Agent as authority

Bad:

```text
agent said it, therefore it is true
```

Correct:

```text
agent output is a received claim requiring evidence and review
```

## 30.5 Activation without kill switch

Bad:

```text
agent keeps operating after context is stale or lease expired
```

Correct:

```text
kill switch stops or suspends authority and requires partial receipt
```

---

## 31. Chapter Insert for Full Encyclopedia

## Chapter: ION Does Not Scale by Making One Agent Infinite

ION’s context problem cannot be solved by asking a single agent to hold everything. The project is too large, too authority-sensitive, and too temporally layered. It contains doctrine, runtime, release evidence, graph law, source-summary rewrite constraints, role history, production gaps, and future agent activation law. A single active context will always compress, omit, or drift.

ION scales differently.

It turns context into graph regions. Those regions can be managed by domain agents, inspected by specialists, and decomposed into contextual node work. Each agent receives a bounded context package, a lease, a task template, an output schema, and a receipt requirement. Fan-out distributes work. Fan-in reconciles evidence. Contradictions are preserved. Nothing becomes canonical merely because an agent said it.

This is not a swarm. It is lawful distributed graph cognition.

The architecture is powerful precisely because it does not pretend subagents are safe by default. They require graph-region custody, activation requests, leases, heartbeats, result receipts, conflict escalation, and kill switches.

The correct current claim is:

```text
ION has the doctrine and architecture for agent/subagent graph operations.
ION has not yet ratified production agent activation authority.
```

That boundary protects the system from the very autonomy it is designing.

---

## 32. Next Artifact

The next encyclopedia artifact should be:

```text
ION_Production_Encyclopedia_v2_0_DAEMON_RUNTIME_DRAFT.md
```

Purpose:

```text
define daemon runtime loop, queue locking, idempotent execution, duplicate detection, crash recovery, resume from receipts, safe mode, operator pause/resume, health checks, audit logs, and why production daemon activation must be graph-layer aware.
```


---

# Volume VII — Daemon Runtime

## 1. Why the Daemon Runtime Matters

ION cannot become production-ready as a collection of manually invoked commands.

The V26–V32 lineage proves a bounded certified path, but much of that path is still operator-driven:

```text
run replay
run doctor
run certification
run evidence bundle
run release candidate
run verifier
```

Production ION needs a daemon because the living context graph should respond to lawful event pressure continuously and safely.

The daemon is the runtime organ that watches for ready graph events, validates them, routes them, executes allowed reactions, records receipts, pauses unsafe actions, and resumes interrupted work.

But a daemon is also dangerous.

A bad daemon can:

```text
commit stale state
replay old events
duplicate writes
overwrite bounded graph state
promote proposals into canon
trigger source-summary rewrites
activate agents without authority
hide failures behind automation
```

Therefore production ION’s daemon must not merely be automatic. It must be lawful.

Core rule:

```text
A production daemon is not an automation loop.
A production daemon is a graph-aware, receipt-backed, idempotent, pausable, recoverable executor of authorized event reactions.
```

---

## 2. Current Runtime Posture

Current state after V33:

```text
certified demo path exists
replay/doctor/certification/evidence/release/verifier path exists
production-readiness gate exists
production daemon authority does not exist
```

The daemon may be described, prototyped, and planned.

It may not yet be treated as production authority.

Current verdict:

```text
[PRODUCTION-GATED]
blocked until V41
```

---

## 3. Daemon Definition

A daemon is a persistent runtime process that:

```text
watches authorized surfaces
detects ready events
validates contracts
checks graph/write authority
selects allowed reactions
executes bounded operations
records receipts
updates reports/indexes where allowed
pauses on uncertainty
recovers after interruption
refuses unauthorized mutation
```

A production daemon must be:

```text
idempotent
queue-locked
graph-layer-aware
contract-bound
receipt-backed
safe-mode capable
operator-pausable
crash-recoverable
auditable
```

---

## 4. Daemon Loop

The canonical daemon loop should be:

```text
scan
→ classify
→ lock
→ validate
→ authorize
→ plan reaction
→ execute
→ receipt
→ post-check
→ release lock
→ report
```

Expanded:

```text
1. scan inbox/queues/registries for ready surfaces
2. classify event type and graph layer
3. acquire queue lock
4. validate template/contract/schema
5. check authority and write class
6. select allowed reaction
7. execute bounded operation
8. emit receipt
9. run post-condition checks
10. release lock
11. update report/index/projection if allowed
12. pause/escalate if any gate fails
```

Production rule:

```text
The daemon must validate before action and receipt after action.
```

---

## 5. Watched Surfaces

A production daemon may eventually watch:

```text
template completion inbox
workflow request inbox
review queue
release-candidate queue
source-summary patch proposals
agent activation requests
daemon health queue
staleness records
open question registry
signal registry
graph conflict registry
```

Before production ratification, watched surfaces should remain bounded and explicit.

Watched surface schema:

```yaml
watched_surface:
  surface_id:
  path:
  surface_type:
  graph_layer:
  event_types: []
  required_contract:
  allowed_reactions: []
  daemon_rights:
    read: true
    lock: true
    propose: false
    bounded_commit: false
    canonical_write: false
    source_rewrite: false
    agent_activation: false
```

Production rule:

```text
The daemon may only watch surfaces declared in a watched-surface registry.
```

---

## 6. Queue Locking

Queue locks prevent duplicate or competing execution.

Lock record:

```yaml
queue_lock:
  lock_id:
  queue_id:
  target_event:
  acquired_by:
  acquired_at:
  expires_at:
  lock_scope:
  heartbeat_required: true
  stale_lock_policy:
    - release_after_expiration
    - require_operator_review
    - resume_from_receipt
```

Queue locking rules:

```text
no event execution without lock
no duplicate lock over same event unless prior lock expired or released
locks must expire
locks must be recoverable
locks must leave evidence
```

Production rule:

```text
No lock, no daemon execution.
```

---

## 7. Idempotent Execution

A daemon action is idempotent when repeating it does not corrupt state.

Idempotency matters because daemons crash, restart, retry, and resume.

Idempotency key:

```yaml
idempotency_key:
  workflow_id:
  event_id:
  target_surface:
  action_type:
  input_hash:
  authority_class:
```

Idempotency record:

```yaml
idempotency_record:
  key:
  first_seen_at:
  status:
    - STARTED
    - COMPLETE
    - FAILED
    - PARTIAL
    - ROLLED_BACK
  output_hash:
  receipt:
  retry_count:
```

Production rule:

```text
A daemon must check idempotency before executing an action.
```

If the action already completed, the daemon returns the prior receipt.

---

## 8. Duplicate Detection

Duplicate detection prevents repeated event pressure from generating multiple writes.

Duplicate sources:

```text
same completed template detected twice
same front-door request replayed
same agent result receipt reprocessed
same source-summary patch proposed twice
same release candidate verified twice
same daemon restart replays unfinished event
```

Duplicate handling:

```text
detect same idempotency key
compare target surface
return existing receipt if complete
escalate if partial/conflicting
refuse overwrite if target already exists
```

Production rule:

```text
Duplicate detection must favor refusal and receipt reuse over silent overwrite.
```

---

## 9. Crash Recovery

The daemon must assume crashes.

Crash recovery requires:

```text
lock state
idempotency state
partial receipts
last successful checkpoint
target surface status
post-condition status
operator-visible recovery report
```

Recovery states:

```text
NOT_STARTED
LOCKED
VALIDATED
AUTHORIZED
EXECUTING
PARTIAL
RECEIPTED
POST_CHECKED
COMPLETE
FAILED
ROLLBACK_REQUIRED
OPERATOR_REVIEW_REQUIRED
```

Recovery record:

```yaml
daemon_recovery_record:
  recovery_id:
  event_id:
  lock_id:
  idempotency_key:
  detected_state:
  last_receipt:
  target_surface_status:
  recovery_action:
    - resume
    - return_existing_receipt
    - rollback
    - escalate
    - operator_review
  emitted_at:
```

Production rule:

```text
A crash must produce recoverable state, not invisible uncertainty.
```

---

## 10. Resume from Receipts

Receipts are the daemon’s memory of lawful action.

Resume from receipts means:

```text
read last receipt
determine completed phase
validate target state
continue from next phase or stop
```

Receipt phases:

```text
scan_receipt
lock_receipt
validation_receipt
authorization_receipt
execution_receipt
commit_receipt
post_check_receipt
release_receipt
failure_receipt
rollback_receipt
```

Production rule:

```text
The daemon resumes from receipts, not from guesswork.
```

---

## 11. Safe Mode

Safe mode is a runtime state where mutation is blocked and only diagnostics, reports, dry-runs, and operator review are allowed.

Safe mode triggers:

```text
graph canon unavailable
authority registry inconsistent
source-summary contradiction critical
queue lock corruption
idempotency conflict
checksum mismatch
release verifier failure
agent activation anomaly
operator command
unknown exception in high-authority path
```

Safe mode rights:

```text
read
diagnose
report
dry-run
verify
bundle evidence
request review
```

Safe mode forbidden:

```text
canonical write
source rewrite
agent activation
daemon-triggered production release
unreviewed bounded commit
```

Production rule:

```text
When in doubt, safe mode outranks progress.
```

---

## 12. Operator Pause/Resume

The daemon must be pausable.

Pause request:

```yaml
daemon_pause_request:
  request_id:
  requested_by:
  scope:
    - all
    - workflow
    - queue
    - graph_region
    - event
  reason:
  immediate: true
  allow_current_action_to_finish: false
```

Resume request:

```yaml
daemon_resume_request:
  request_id:
  requested_by:
  scope:
  required_checks: []
  resume_from_receipts: true
```

Production rule:

```text
A production daemon must obey operator pause and resume through receipts.
```

---

## 13. Health Checks

Health checks show whether the daemon is safe to run.

Health check categories:

```text
filesystem access
registry validity
template contract validity
queue lock validity
idempotency store validity
graph write authority validity
release verifier availability
safe mode status
watcher status
receipt emission status
```

Health report:

```yaml
daemon_health_report:
  report_id:
  emitted_at:
  status:
    - HEALTHY
    - DEGRADED
    - SAFE_MODE
    - BLOCKED
  checks:
    - check_id:
      status:
      detail:
  required_action:
```

Production rule:

```text
A daemon must not start high-authority operations when health is BLOCKED.
```

---

## 14. Audit Log

The daemon audit log records every significant daemon action.

Audit events:

```text
scan
lock acquire
lock release
validation pass/fail
authority check pass/fail
reaction selected
execution started
execution complete
receipt emitted
safe mode entered
safe mode exited
pause
resume
crash recovery
duplicate detected
unauthorized action refused
```

Audit event schema:

```yaml
daemon_audit_event:
  event_id:
  daemon_id:
  timestamp:
  action:
  target:
  graph_layer:
  authority_class:
  result:
  receipt:
  operator_visible: true
```

Production rule:

```text
A daemon action without audit trace is not production-valid.
```

---

## 15. Dry-Run Mode

Dry-run mode executes all checks without mutation.

Dry-run should report:

```text
events that would run
locks that would be acquired
reactions that would be selected
writes that would be attempted
authority checks that would pass/fail
receipts that would be emitted
risks detected
```

Production rule:

```text
Every high-authority daemon workflow should support dry-run.
```

---

## 16. Graph-Layer Awareness

The daemon must understand graph write classes.

Relevant write classes:

```text
W0 read only
W1 working graph write
W2 proposal graph write
W3 review graph write
W4 bounded commit graph write
W5 canonical context graph write
W6 source graph rewrite
W7 agent memory/context graph activation write
```

Current after V33:

```text
W0 allowed
W1 allowed in bounded runtime contexts
W2 allowed where templates permit
W3 allowed where review surfaces permit
W4 allowed only in bounded demo/proven surfaces
W5 blocked
W6 blocked
W7 blocked
```

Production rule:

```text
The daemon must refuse W5/W6/W7 until ratified authority exists.
```

---

## 17. Daemon and Source Summary Rewrite

The daemon may eventually process source-summary rewrite proposals.

Before V37–V38:

```text
source summary commit is blocked
daemon may only report/propose/dry-run
```

After V37–V38:

```text
daemon may process reviewed source-summary commits only if:
  graph canon exists
  source-summary rewrite authority exists
  LAND review exists
  rollback receipt is generated
  dependent context packages are invalidated
```

Production rule:

```text
Daemon automation must not bypass source-summary rewrite review.
```

---

## 18. Daemon and Agent Activation

The daemon may eventually process agent activation requests.

Before V39–V40:

```text
agent activation is blocked
daemon may only record or route activation proposals
```

After V39–V40:

```text
daemon may activate bounded agents/subagents only if:
  activation request approved
  context package exists
  lease exists
  heartbeat configured
  kill switch exists
  result receipt required
  spawn depth and budget are enforced
```

Production rule:

```text
Daemon automation must not spawn agents before agent activation law.
```

---

## 19. Daemon and Release Engineering

The daemon may eventually assist release engineering.

Possible actions:

```text
collect evidence
run verifier
emit readiness report
package release candidate
detect checksum mismatch
enter safe mode on tamper failure
```

Production rule:

```text
The daemon may package evidence only under release authority and must not certify itself without independent verifier evidence.
```

---

## 20. Daemon and Front Door

The front door may request daemon actions, but must not collapse into the daemon.

Correct relation:

```text
Persona Interface receives user intent
Relay translates intent into graph-native packet
Steward routes packet
Daemon executes authorized event reactions
Relay packages result
Persona renders response
```

Production rule:

```text
The front door asks; the daemon executes only authorized graph reactions.
```

---

## 21. Daemon Failure Modes

Production daemon design must explicitly defend against:

```text
double execution
stale event replay
lock leak
partial commit
receipt loss
silent overwrite
authority bypass
unsafe source rewrite
unsafe agent spawn
graph-layer confusion
safe mode failure
operator pause ignored
checksum mismatch ignored
front-door role collapse
```

Each failure mode needs a test.

---

```text
ION/02_architecture/ION_DAEMON_RUNTIME_PROTOCOL.md

ION/02_architecture/IDEMPOTENT_EVENT_EXECUTION_PROTOCOL.md
ION/02_architecture/DAEMON_CRASH_RECOVERY_PROTOCOL.md
ION/02_architecture/DAEMON_SAFE_MODE_PROTOCOL.md

ION/tests/test_kernel_daemon_audit_log.py
```

## 23. V41 Acceptance Criteria

The production daemon is minimally production-candidate when:

```text
1. watched surfaces are declared in registry
2. daemon acquires lock before action
3. daemon checks idempotency before action
4. daemon validates contracts before action
5. daemon checks graph write authority before action
6. daemon emits receipts after action
7. daemon can resume from receipts
8. daemon can detect duplicate events
9. daemon can recover after simulated crash
10. daemon can enter safe mode
11. daemon obeys operator pause
12. daemon health check blocks unsafe high-authority work
13. daemon refuses W5/W6/W7 by default
14. daemon refuses source-summary commit before V37–V38 authority
15. daemon refuses agent activation before V39–V40 authority
16. daemon audit log records all significant actions
```

---

## 24. Anti-Patterns

## 24.1 Automation as authority

Bad:

```text
daemon did it, therefore it was lawful
```

Correct:

```text
daemon may execute only what authority permits and receipts prove
```

## 24.2 Event replay without idempotency

Bad:

```text
restart daemon and rerun all detected events
```

Correct:

```text
check idempotency, receipts, locks, and target state
```

## 24.3 Silent recovery

Bad:

```text
crash happened, daemon guessed where to continue
```

Correct:

```text
daemon resumes from receipts or escalates
```

## 24.4 Safe mode bypass

Bad:

```text
safe mode blocks too much, so daemon bypasses it
```

Correct:

```text
safe mode outranks progress
```

## 24.5 Daemon-spawned swarm

Bad:

```text
daemon detects complex task and spawns subagents automatically
```

Correct:

```text
daemon routes activation proposal until agent activation law exists
```

---

## 25. Chapter Insert for Full Encyclopedia

## Chapter: The Daemon Is Not an Automation Script

ION’s daemon is often tempting to describe as “the automation system.” That is too weak and too dangerous.

A normal automation script watches for a trigger and performs an action. ION’s daemon must do something stricter. It must interpret event pressure inside a governed graph. It must know which graph layer is being touched, what authority class applies, whether a queue lock exists, whether the action is idempotent, whether the target has already been written, whether a receipt exists, whether safe mode is active, and whether the action would cross into canonical graph write, source-summary rewrite, or agent activation.

The daemon is not allowed to make progress at any cost. It is allowed to make lawful progress.

That means the daemon must be able to stop. It must pause. It must enter safe mode. It must refuse duplicate writes. It must resume from receipts instead of guessing. It must leave an audit trail. It must make unsafe work visible rather than silently completing it.

The current certified demo line proves that ION can execute bounded workflows through operator commands and verified evidence. It does not yet prove production daemon safety.

The correct current claim is:

```text
ION has a certified evented workflow spine.
ION has not yet ratified a production daemon runtime.
```

That boundary is what lets the daemon eventually become trustworthy.

---

## 26. Next Artifact

The next encyclopedia artifact should be:

```text
ION_Production_Encyclopedia_v2_0_FRONT_DOOR_PRODUCT_SURFACE_DRAFT.md
```

Purpose:

```text
define production front-door product surfaces: Persona Interface, Relay, Steward, CLI/API/chat entrypoints, operator traceability, review queue visibility, artifact browser, user-facing response packages, and how front-door productization remains separate from daemon authority.
```


---

# Volume VIII — Front Door Product Surface

## 1. Why the Front Door Matters

ION cannot become useful as a production system if its power remains hidden inside internal protocols, runtime modules, and release artifacts.

The front door is where a user, operator, reviewer, or external system meets ION.

A production front door must answer:

```text
What can I ask ION to do?
What did ION understand?
What graph-native work was created?
What authority did the work require?
What changed?
What was refused?
What is waiting for review?
What evidence exists?
What can be verified?
What can be rolled back?
What remains blocked?
```

This is not merely UI. It is governance made visible.

The front door is the product expression of ION’s law.

---

## 2. The Core Front-Door Chain

ION’s front-door chain is:

```text
User / External Caller
→ Persona Interface
→ Relay
→ Steward
→ Workflow / Daemon / Review / Release Surface
→ Steward return
→ Relay return package
→ Persona Interface response
→ User / External Caller
```

This chain must not collapse.

Each role exists to prevent a different failure.

```text
Persona Interface prevents graph-native machinery from leaking directly to the user.
Relay prevents semantic-boundary confusion.
Steward prevents user-facing language from becoming orchestration authority.
Daemon prevents orchestration from becoming arbitrary automation.
Review surfaces prevent proposals from becoming truth.
Release/verifier surfaces prevent demos from becoming unverifiable claims.
```

---

## 3. Persona Interface

## 3.1 Definition

Persona Interface is the user-facing renderer and intake surface.

It receives human-facing input and renders human-facing output.

It does not own graph-native orchestration.

## 3.2 Responsibilities

Persona Interface should:

```text
receive user intent
preserve user-visible context
ask clarification when required
render ION’s boundaries plainly
show what was done
show what was refused
show what remains blocked
present links/artifacts/evidence
translate graph-native status into human-readable form
```

## 3.3 Forbidden responsibilities

Persona Interface must not:

```text
grant authority
mutate graph canon
rewrite source summaries
spawn agents
bypass Relay
bypass Steward
claim production readiness
hide uncertainty
smooth contradictions
```

## 3.4 Product rule

```text
Persona Interface speaks to the user.
It does not become the system’s authority.
```

---

## 4. Relay

## 4.1 Definition

Relay is the semantic-boundary translator.

It converts:

```text
user-facing intent
↔ graph-native packet
↔ runtime result
↔ user-facing response package
```

## 4.2 Responsibilities

Relay should:

```text
separate user language from graph-language
preserve ambiguity
mark missing authority
mark required review
package return evidence
avoid role mixing
prevent front-door overclaim
```

## 4.3 Forbidden responsibilities

Relay must not:

```text
decide final orchestration route by itself
rewrite authority classes
collapse proposal into command
treat natural-language confidence as graph proof
grant daemon execution rights
```

## 4.4 Product rule

```text
Relay translates boundaries; it does not dissolve them.
```

---

## 5. Steward

## 5.1 Definition

Steward is the current orchestration truename.

Steward receives graph-native packets and routes lawful work.

## 5.2 Responsibilities

Steward should:

```text
select workflow path
request review where required
route to daemon only when authorized
route to certification/release/verifier surfaces where appropriate
preserve production-readiness boundaries
refuse unauthorized mutation
return structured result to Relay
```

## 5.3 Forbidden responsibilities before production ratification

Before V42 and related gates, Steward must not:

```text
grant canonical graph writes
grant source-summary rewrites
spawn agents autonomously
override daemon safe mode
bypass review
claim production readiness
```

## 5.4 Product rule

```text
Steward routes graph-native work.
It does not replace graph canon, daemon safety, or review authority.
```

---

## 6. Product Entry Points

Production ION should eventually have multiple front-door entry points.

```text
chat
CLI
API
IDE
custom GPT / bundle carrier
operator dashboard
artifact browser
review queue
release verifier
```

Each entry point must still preserve the same semantic chain.

---

## 7. Chat Entry Point

## 7.1 Purpose

The chat entry point is the most natural human surface.

It should allow:

```text
ask for project state
request summary refresh
request doctrine evolution proposal
ask what is blocked
ask what evidence exists
ask what changed
request release verification
request production-readiness report
```

## 7.2 Requirements

Chat must show:

```text
action taken
authority used
evidence produced
blocked claims
next lawful move
artifacts
uncertainties
```

## 7.3 Forbidden

Chat must not:

```text
silently execute high-authority mutation
hide daemon/refusal state
present demo status as production status
collapse confidence into truth
```

---

## 8. CLI Entry Point

## 8.1 Purpose

CLI is the operator/developer control surface.

It should support:

```bash
ion status
ion readiness
ion verify-release
ion doctor --workflow <id>
ion replay --workflow <id>
ion certify --workflow <id>
ion evidence-bundle --workflow <id>
ion release-candidate --workflow <id>
ion daemon health
ion daemon safe-mode
ion queue inspect
ion review list
```

## 8.2 CLI requirements

CLI commands should provide:

```text
machine-readable JSON mode
human-readable summary mode
exit codes
receipt path
artifact path
boundary claims
refusal reasons
```

## 8.3 Exit code policy

Recommended:

```text
0 = success / verified / ready within declared scope
1 = execution error
2 = blocked / not ready / failed verdict
3 = invalid authority or unsafe request
4 = missing source/evidence
5 = review required
```

---

## 9. API Entry Point

## 9.1 Purpose

API is the external integration surface.

It should expose governed operations to other systems.

Candidate endpoints:

```text
POST /front-door/turn
POST /workflow/request
GET  /workflow/{id}/status
POST /workflow/{id}/doctor
POST /workflow/{id}/certify
POST /release-candidate/verify
GET  /readiness
GET  /queue/review
POST /review/{id}/verdict
GET  /artifact/{id}
```

## 9.2 API requirements

API must include:

```text
request ID
idempotency key
authority class
caller identity
requested rights
dry-run option
receipt output
artifact links
review requirement
```

## 9.3 Forbidden

API must not expose raw mutation without authority gates.

Production rule:

```text
Every API mutation request is a graph-native proposal or authorized workflow request, not an arbitrary write.
```

---

## 10. IDE Entry Point

## 10.1 Purpose

The IDE entry point is where builder agents and human operators work with files.

It should expose:

```text
current file authority
template contract
graph region
staleness status
related receipts
related tests
allowed actions
blocked actions
```

## 10.2 Requirements

IDE surface should show:

```text
why a file is safe or unsafe to edit
what templates govern it
what graph region it belongs to
what summaries depend on it
what agents have touched it
what release artifacts include it
```

## 10.3 Forbidden

IDE integration must not let an agent edit protected files merely because it has file access.

Production rule:

```text
Filesystem access is not mutation authority.
```

---

## 11. Operator Dashboard

## 11.1 Purpose

The operator dashboard is the visibility surface for production ION.

It should show:

```text
system readiness
daemon health
safe mode status
review queue
open contradictions
stale summaries
active leases
release candidates
verification status
blocked production gates
recent receipts
```

## 11.2 Dashboard rule

```text
The dashboard must make refusal visible.
```

A system that hides refusals will train users to believe nothing is blocked.

---

## 12. Review Queue

## 12.1 Purpose

The review queue is where proposals become reviewed outcomes.

It should support:

```text
LAND
HOLD
ESCALATE
REJECT
NEEDS_SOURCE
NEEDS_CONTRADICTION_SCAN
NEEDS_HUMAN_REVIEW
```

## 12.2 Review queue item

```yaml
review_queue_item:
  review_id:
  proposal_id:
  graph_region:
  authority_class:
  requested_rights:
  evidence_refs: []
  contradictions: []
  risk_level:
  recommended_verdict:
  required_reviewer:
  deadline:
```

## 12.3 Production rule

```text
Review must be explicit, recorded, and receipt-backed.
```

---

## 13. Artifact Browser

## 13.1 Purpose

The artifact browser lets users inspect ION’s evidence.

It should browse:

```text
receipts
replay reports
doctor reports
certification reports
evidence bundles
release candidates
verification reports
production-readiness reports
ratification reports
summary patches
rollback receipts
agent result receipts
daemon audit logs
```

## 13.2 Requirements

Every artifact should show:

```text
artifact ID
type
authority class
created by
created at
source paths
hash
related graph nodes
related workflow
current status
superseded by / supersedes
verification status
```

## 13.3 Production rule

```text
Evidence must be navigable, not buried.
```

---

## 14. User-Facing Response Package

All front-door responses should be built from a structured response package.

```yaml
front_door_response_package:
  response_id:
  request_id:
  user_visible_summary:
  action_status:
    - COMPLETED
    - BLOCKED
    - PARTIAL
    - REVIEW_REQUIRED
    - FAILED
  authority_used:
  boundaries:
    production_ready_claimed: false
    canonical_write_performed: false
    source_rewrite_performed: false
    agent_activation_performed: false
  artifacts: []
  receipts: []
  review_items: []
  blocked_reasons: []
  next_lawful_moves: []
  uncertainties: []
```

Production rule:

```text
A response should not merely say what happened. It should say what authority was used and what authority was not used.
```

---

## 15. Operator Traceability

Traceability answers:

```text
why did ION do this?
```

A trace should include:

```text
user request
Relay packet
Steward route
workflow selected
authority checked
daemon action if any
review verdict if any
commit if any
receipt
artifact
return package
```

Trace schema:

```yaml
operator_trace:
  trace_id:
  request_id:
  chain:
    - stage:
      actor:
      input:
      output:
      authority:
      receipt:
  final_status:
  artifacts: []
```

Production rule:

```text
No front-door action without traceability.
```

---

## 16. Error and Refusal UX

ION must explain refusals.

Refusal categories:

```text
missing authority
missing source
graph canon blocked
source-summary rewrite blocked
agent activation blocked
daemon safe mode
review required
contradiction found
checksum mismatch
not production-ready
```

Refusal package:

```yaml
front_door_refusal:
  refusal_id:
  request_id:
  category:
  reason:
  blocked_authority:
  required_gate:
  available_safe_action:
  evidence_refs: []
```

Production rule:

```text
Refusal is a product feature.
```

---

## 17. Front Door and Daemon Boundary

The front door may request daemon actions, but does not become the daemon.

Correct relation:

```text
front door creates/frames request
Relay translates
Steward routes
daemon executes only if authorized
front door renders result
```

Forbidden relation:

```text
front door directly mutates graph state
front door directly spawns agents
front door directly rewrites summaries
front door bypasses daemon locks
```

Production rule:

```text
The front door asks through law; it does not act outside it.
```

---

## 18. Front Door and Release Engineering

The front door should expose release engineering.

User/operator requests:

```text
verify current release candidate
show certification report
download evidence bundle
show checksum status
compare current zip to manifest
explain why release is blocked
```

The front door must preserve:

```text
verified demo release candidate != production release
```

Production rule:

```text
Release status must be shown with exact scope.
```

---

## 19. Front Door and Production Readiness

The front door should be able to answer:

```text
Is ION production-ready?
```

Current answer:

```text
No. Current verdict: NOT_PRODUCTION_READY.
```

It should then explain:

```text
certified demo baseline exists
production-readiness gate exists
critical gaps remain
next gate is V34
```

Production rule:

```text
The front door must never translate NOT_PRODUCTION_READY into marketing optimism.
```

---

## 20. Front Door and Agent Activation

Before V39–V40, the front door may receive requests involving agents, but must route them as proposals.

Allowed:

```text
draft agent activation proposal
show required context package
show blocked activation reason
show future activation law
```

Blocked:

```text
spawn subagents
grant graph-region custody
grant source rewrite rights
grant canonical write rights
```

Production rule:

```text
User request does not grant agent activation authority.
```

---

## 21. Front Door and Source Summary Rewrite

Before V37–V38, the front door may receive summary rewrite requests, but must not perform canonical rewrite.

Allowed:

```text
draft summary
draft patch proposal
identify required provenance
run contradiction scan proposal
show blocked commit reason
```

Blocked:

```text
canonical source-summary overwrite
source-summary patch commit
silent context package refresh
```

Production rule:

```text
User request does not bypass source-summary rewrite authority.
```

---

## 22. Front Door and Graph Canon

Before V36, the front door may discuss graph canon and propose graph changes, but must not claim canonical graph write authority.

Allowed:

```text
show graph-canon gap
draft graph proposal
show bounded graph-state commit
show release/evidence graph
```

Blocked:

```text
global canonical graph mutation
claiming bounded commit is canon
treating index projection as canon
```

Production rule:

```text
The front door must preserve graph-layer distinctions.
```

---

V42 should create production front-door surfaces.

```text
ION/02_architecture/FRONT_DOOR_PRODUCT_SURFACE_PROTOCOL.md
ION/02_architecture/FRONT_DOOR_TRACEABILITY_PROTOCOL.md
ION/02_architecture/FRONT_DOOR_REVIEW_QUEUE_PROTOCOL.md
ION/02_architecture/FRONT_DOOR_ARTIFACT_BROWSER_PROTOCOL.md

ION/04_packages/kernel/front_door_trace.py

ION/04_packages/kernel/front_door_response_package.py

ION/tests/test_kernel_front_door_trace.py
ION/tests/test_kernel_front_door_review_queue.py
ION/tests/test_kernel_front_door_artifact_browser.py
ION/tests/test_kernel_front_door_response_package.py
```
